The Elizabeth Sanders quotation is in her book, [3mRoots of Reform: Farmers, Workers, and the American State, 1877โ1917 [0m(Chicago: 
University of Chicago Press, 1999), 169. The later quotation is on 158. Sandersโ book articulates the thesis that Progressivismโs 
legislative muscle lay in agrarian parts of the country. Party affiliationโPopulist, Democratic, or insurgent Republicanโwas less 
important than connections to the agrarian economy and the concentration of political strength in such districts. In urban or 
mixed districts, she argues, the interests of labor (the natural ally of agrarians) were diluted by competing forces. Close 
students may want to know that Sandersโ interpretation is recent and, to me, convincingly solves the problem of interparty, 
intersectional, urban [3mand [0mrural support for Progressivism. Earlier interpretations such as Robert Wiebeโs widely read [3mThe Search 
for Order [0m(1967) stress reformersโ search for social control of both the new industrial capitalism and the mass of potentially 
radical people. Others such as Martin Sklar had written in the 1960s, from a Marxist perspective, that Progressive-era reform was 
a cover for business interests. This became known as the โcorporate-liberalโ interpretation. In [3mThe Corporate Reconstruction of 
American Capitalism, 1890โ1916 [0m(1988), Sklar explored more subtly and in more detail how capitalism moved โfrom the competitive to 
the corporate stage of its developmentโ by 1914.
